In short, the union speech at issue in this case is overwhelmingly of substantial public concern.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">C</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The only remaining question under <em>Pickering </em>is whether<em> </em>the State's proffered interests justify the heavy burden that agency fees inflict on nonmembers' First Amendment interests.  We have already addressed the state interests asserted in <em>Abood</em>--promoting "labor peace" and avoiding free riders, see <em>supra</em>, at 11-18--and we will not repeat that analysis.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In <em>Harris</em> and this case, defenders of <em>Abood</em> have asserted a different state interest--in the words of the <em>Harris</em> dissent, the State's "interest in bargaining with an adequately funded exclusive bargaining agent."  573 U.&nbsp;S., at ___<em> </em>(<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kagan</small></span>, J., dissenting) (slip op., at 7); see also <em>post</em>, at 6-7 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kagan, J.</small></span>, dissenting).  This was not "the interest <em>Abood</em> recognized and protected," <em>Harris</em>, <em>supra</em>, at ___ (slip op., at 7) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kagan, J.,</small></span> dissenting), and, in any event, it is insufficient.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Although the dissent would accept without any serious  independent evaluation the State's assertion that the absence of agency fees would cripple public-sector unions and thus impair the efficiency of government operations, see <em>post</em>, at 8-9, 11, ample experience, as we have noted, <em>supra</em>, at 12, shows that this is questionable.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Especially in light of the more rigorous form of <em>Pickering </em>analysis that would apply in this context, see <em>supra</em>, at 23-25, the balance tips decisively in favor of the employees' free speech rights.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 23" name="FRopinion1.23" href="#FNopinion1.23">23</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;We readily acknowledge, as <em>Pickering </em>did, that "the State has interests as an employer in regulating the speech of its employees that differ significantly from those it possesses in connection with regulation of the speech of the citizenry in general."  391 U.&nbsp;S., at 568.  Our analysis is consistent with that principle.  The exacting scrutiny standard we apply in this case was developed in the context of commercial speech, another area where the government has traditionally enjoyed greater-than-usual power to regulate speech.  See <em>supra</em>, at 10.  It is also not disputed that the State may require that a union serve as exclusive bargaining agent for its employees--itself a significant impingement on associational freedoms that would not be tolerated in other contexts.  We simply draw the line at allowing the government to go further still and require all employees to support the union irrespective of whether they share its views.  Nothing in the <em>Pickering </em>line of cases requires us to uphold every speech restriction the government imposes as an employer.  See <em>Pickering</em>,<em> supra</em>, at 564-566 (holding teacher's dismissal for criticizing school board unconstitutional); <em>Rankin</em> v. <em>McPherson</em>, 483 U.&nbsp;S. 378, 392 (1987) (holding clerical employee's dismissal for supporting assassination attempt on President unconstitutional); <em>Treasury Employees</em>, 513 U.&nbsp;S., at 477 (holding federal-employee honoraria ban unconstitutional).</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">VI</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;For the reasons given above, we conclude that public-sector agency-shop arrangements violate the First Amendment, and <em>Abood</em> erred in concluding otherwise.  There remains the question whether <em>stare decisis</em> nonetheless counsels against overruling <em>Abood</em>.  It does not.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"<em>Stare decisis</em> is the preferred course because it promotes the evenhanded, predictable, and consistent development of legal principles, fosters reliance on judicial decisions, and contributes to the actual and perceived integrity of the judicial process."  <em>Payne</em> v. <em>Tennessee</em>, 501 U.&nbsp;S. 808, 827 (1991).  We will not overturn a past decision unless there are strong grounds for doing so.  <em>United States</em> v. <em>International Business Machines Corp.</em>, 517 U.&nbsp;S. 843, 855-856 (1996); <em>Citizens United</em>, 558 U.&nbsp;S., at 377 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Roberts, C.&nbsp;J.</small></span>, concurring).  But as we have often recognized, <em>stare decisis</em> is "&nbsp;'not an inexorable command.'&nbsp;"  <em>Pearson</em> v. <em>Callahan</em>, 555 U.&nbsp;S. 223, 233 (2009); see also <em>Lawrence</em> v. <em>Texas</em>, 539 U.&nbsp;S. 558, 577 (2003); <em>State Oil Co.</em> v. <em>Khan</em>, 522 U.&nbsp;S. 3, 20 (1997); <em>Agostini</em> v. <em>Felton</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S. 203, 235 (1997); <em>Seminole Tribe of Fla.</em> v. <em>Florida</em>, 517 U.&nbsp;S. 44, 63 (1996); <em>Payne</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 828.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The doctrine "is at its weakest when we interpret the Constitution because our interpretation can be altered only by constitutional amendment or by overruling our prior decisions."  <em>Agostini</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 235.  And <em>stare decisis</em> applies with perhaps least force of all to decisions that wrongly denied First Amendment rights: "This Court has not hesitated to overrule decisions offensive to the First Amendment (a fixed star in our constitutional constellation, if there is one)."  <em>Federal Election Comm'n</em> v. <em>Wisconsin Right to Life, Inc.</em>, 551 U.&nbsp;S. 449, 500 (2007) (Scalia, J., concurring in part and concurring in judgment) (internal quotation marks omitted); see also <em>Citizens United</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 362-365 (overruling <em>Austin</em>, 494 U.&nbsp;S. 652); <em>Barnette</em>, 319 U.&nbsp;S., at 642 (overruling <em>Minersville School Dist.</em> v. <em>Gobitis</em>, 310 U.&nbsp;S. 586 (1940)).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Our cases identify factors that should be taken into account in deciding whether to overrule a past decision.  Five of these are most important here: the quality of <em>Abood</em>'s reasoning, the workability of the rule it established, its consistency with other related decisions, developments since the decision was handed down, and reliance on the decision.  After analyzing these factors, we conclude that <em>stare decisis</em> does not require us to retain <em>Abood</em>.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">A</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;An important factor in determining whether a precedent should be overruled is the quality of its reasoning, see <em>Citizens United</em>, 558 U.&nbsp;S., at 363-364; <em>id.</em>, at 382-385 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Roberts, C.&nbsp;J.</small></span>, concurring); <em>Lawrence</em>, 539 U.&nbsp;S., at 577-578, and as we explained in <em>Harris</em>, <em>Abood</em> was poorly reasoned, see 573 U.&nbsp;S., at ___-___ (slip op., at 17-20).  We will summarize, but not repeat, <em>Harris</em>'s lengthy discussion of the issue.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Abood</em> went wrong at the start when it concluded that two prior decisions, <em>Railway Employes</em> v. <em>Hanson</em>, 351 U.&nbsp;S. 225 (1956), and <em>Machinists</em> v. <em>Street</em>, 367 U.&nbsp;S. 740 (1961), "appear[ed] to require validation of the agency-shop agreement before [the Court]."  431 U.&nbsp;S., at 226.  Properly understood, those decisions did no such thing.  Both cases<em> </em>involved Congress's "<em>bare authorization</em>" of <em>private-sector</em> union shops under the Railway Labor Act.  <em>Street</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 749 (emphasis added).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 24" name="FRopinion1.24" href="#FNopinion1.24">24</a></sup>  <em>Abood</em> failed to appreciate that a very different First Amendment question arises when a State <em>requires</em> its employees to pay agency fees.  See <em>Harris</em>,<em> supra</em>, at ___ (slip op., at 17).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Moreover, neither <em>Hanson</em> nor <em>Street </em>gave careful consideration to the First Amendment.  In <em>Hanson</em>,<em> </em>the primary questions were whether Congress exceeded its power under the Commerce Clause or violated substantive due process by authorizing private union-shop arrangements under the Commerce and Due Process Clauses.  351 U.&nbsp;S., at 233-235.  After deciding those questions, the Court summarily dismissed what was essentially a facial First Amendment challenge, noting that the record did not substantiate the challengers' claim.  <em>Id.</em>, at 238; see <em>Harris</em>, <em>supra</em>, at ___ (slip op., at 17).  For its part, <em>Street</em> was decided as a matter of statutory construction, and so did not reach any constitutional issue.  367 U.&nbsp;S., at 749-750, 768-769.  <em>Abood</em> nevertheless took the view that <em>Hanson </em>and <em>Street</em> "all but decided" the important free speech issue that was before the Court.  <em>Harris</em>, 573 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (slip op., at 17).  As we said in <em>Harris</em>, "[s]urely a First Amendment issue of this importance deserved better treatment."  <em>Ibid.</em></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Abood</em>'s unwarranted reliance on <em>Hanson</em> and <em>Street</em> appears to have contributed to another mistake: <em>Abood </em>judged the constitutionality of public-sector agency fees under a deferential standard that finds no support in our free speech cases.  (As noted, <em>supra</em>, at 10-11, today's dissent makes the same fundamental mistake.)  <em>Abood</em> did not independently evaluate the strength of the government interests that were said to support the challenged agency-fee provision; nor did it ask how well that provision actually promoted those interests or whether they could have been adequately served without impinging so heavily on the free speech rights of nonmembers.  Rather, <em>Abood</em> followed <em>Hanson</em> and <em>Street</em>, which it interpreted<em> </em>as having deferred to "<em>the legislative assessment</em> of the important contribution of the union shop to the system of labor relations established by Congress."  431 U.&nbsp;S., at 222 (emphasis added).  But <em>Hanson</em> deferred to that judgment in deciding the Commerce Clause and substantive due process questions that were the focus of the case.  Such deference to legislative judgments is inappropriate in deciding free speech issues.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;If <em>Abood</em> had considered whether agency fees were actually needed to serve the asserted state interests, it might not have made the serious mistake of assuming that one of those interests--"labor peace"--demanded, not only that a single union be designated as the exclusive representative of all the employees in the relevant unit, but also that nonmembers be required to pay agency fees.  Deferring to a perceived legislative judgment, <em>Abood</em> failed to see that the designation of a union as exclusive representative and the imposition of agency fees are not inextricably linked.  See <em>supra</em>, at 11-12; <em>Harris</em>, <em>supra</em>, at ___ (slip op., at 31).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Abood</em> also did not sufficiently take into account the difference between the effects of agency fees in public- and private-sector collective bargaining.  The challengers in <em>Abood </em>argued that collective bargaining with a government employer, unlike collective bargaining in the private sector, involves "inherently 'political'&nbsp;" speech.  431 U.&nbsp;S., at 226.  The Court did not dispute that characterization, and in fact conceded that "decisionmaking by a public employer is above all a political process" driven more by policy concerns than economic ones.  <em>Id.</em>, at 228; see <em>id.</em>, at 228-231.  But (again invoking <em>Hanson</em>), the <em>Abood</em> Court asserted that public employees do not have "weightier First Amendment interest[s]" against compelled speech than do private employees.  <em>Id.</em>, at 229.  That missed the point.  Assuming for the sake of argument that the First Amendment applies at all to private-sector agency-shop arrangements, the individual interests at stake still differ.  "In the public sector, core issues such as wages, pensions, and benefits are important political issues, but that is generally not so in the private sector."  <em>Harris</em>, 573 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (slip op., at 17).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Overlooking the importance of this distinction, "<em>Abood</em> failed to appreciate the conceptual difficulty of distinguishing in public-sector cases between union expenditures that are made for collective-bargaining purposes and those that are made to achieve political ends."  <em>Id.</em>, at ___ (slip op., at 18).  Likewise, "<em>Abood</em> does not seem to have anticipated the magnitude of the practical administrative problems that would result in attempting to classify  public-sector union expenditures as either 'chargeable'&nbsp;.&nbsp;.&nbsp;.&nbsp;or nonchargeable."  <em>Ibid.</em>  Nor did <em>Abood</em> "foresee the practical problems that would face objecting nonmembers."  <em>Id.</em>, at ___ (slip op., at 19).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In sum, as detailed in <em>Harris</em>, <em>Abood</em> was not well  reasoned.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 25" name="FRopinion1.25" href="#FNopinion1.25">25</a></sup></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">B</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Another relevant consideration in the <em>stare decisis</em> calculus is the workability of the precedent in question, <em>Montejo</em> v. <em>Louisiana</em>, 556 U.&nbsp;S. 778, 792 (2009), and that factor also weighs against <em>Abood</em>.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">1</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Abood</em>'s line between chargeable and nonchargeable union expenditures has proved to be impossible to draw with precision.  We tried to give the line some definition in <em>Lehnert</em>.  There, a majority of the Court adopted a three-part test requiring that chargeable expenses (1) be "&nbsp;'germane'&nbsp;" to collective bargaining, (2) be "justified" by the government's labor-peace and free-rider interests, and (3) not add "significantly" to the burden on free speech, 500 U.&nbsp;S., at 519, but the Court splintered over the application of this test, see <em>id.</em>, at 519-522 (plurality opinion);<em> id.</em>, at 533-534 (Marshall, J., concurring in part and dissenting in part).  That division was not surprising.  As the <em>Lehnert </em>dissenters aptly observed, each part of the majority's test "involves a substantial judgment call," <em>id.</em>, at 551 (opinion of Scalia<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>, J.</small></span>), rendering the test "altogether malleable" and "no[t] principled," <em>id.</em>, at 563 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kennedy</small></span>, J., concurring in judgment in part and dissenting in part).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Justice Scalia presciently warned that <em>Lehnert</em>'s amorphous standard<em> </em>would invite "perpetua[l] give-it-a-try litigation," <em>id.</em>, at 551, and the Court's experience with union lobbying expenses illustrates the point.  The <em>Lehnert </em>plurality held that money spent on lobbying for increased education funding was not chargeable.  <em>Id.</em>, at 519-522.  But Justice Marshall--applying the same three-prong test--reached precisely the opposite conclusion.  <em>Id.</em>, at 533-542.  And <em>Lehnert</em> failed to settle the matter; States and unions have continued to "give it a try" ever since.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In <em>Knox</em>, for example, we confronted a union's claim that the costs of lobbying the legislature and the electorate about a ballot measure were chargeable expenses under <em>Lehnert</em>.  See Brief for Respondent in <em>Knox</em> v. <em>Service Employees</em>, O.&nbsp;T. 2011, No. 10-1121, pp.&nbsp;48-53.  The Court rejected this claim out of hand, 567 U.&nbsp;S., at 320-321, but the dissent refused to do so, <em>id.</em>, at 336 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Breyer, J.</small></span>).  And in the present case, nonmembers are required to pay for unspecified "[l]obbying" expenses and for "[s]ervices" that "may ultimately inure to the benefit of the members of the local bargaining unit."  App. to Pet. for Cert. 31a-32a.  That formulation is broad enough to encompass just about anything that the union might choose to do.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Respondents agree that <em>Abood</em>'s chargeable-nonchargeable line suffers from "a vagueness problem," that it sometimes "allows what it shouldn't allow," and that "a firm[er] line c[ould] be drawn."  Tr. of Oral Arg. 47-48.  They therefore argue that we should "consider revisiting" this part of <em>Abood</em>.  Tr. of Oral Arg. 66; see Brief for Union Respondent 46-47; Brief for State Respondents 30.  This concession only underscores the reality that <em>Abood</em> has proved unworkable: Not even the parties defending agency fees support the line that it has taken this Court over 40 years to draw.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">2</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Objecting employees also face a daunting and expensive task if they wish to challenge union chargeability determinations.  While <em>Hudson </em>requires a union to provide nonmembers with "sufficient information to gauge the propriety of the union's fee," 475 U.&nbsp;S., at 306, the <em>Hudson</em> notice in the present case and in others that have come before us do not begin to permit a nonmember to make such a determination.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In this case, the notice lists categories of expenses and sets out the amount in each category that is said to be attributable to chargeable and nonchargeable expenses.  Here are some examples regarding the Union respondent's expenditures:</p>
<p>Category</p>

<p>Total Expense</p>

<p>Chargeable Expense</p>

<p>Salary and</p>
<p>Benefits</p>

<p>$14,718,708</p>

<p>$11,830,230</p>

<p>Office Printing, Supplies, and Advertising</p>

<p>$148,272</p>

<p>$127,959</p>

<p>Postage and Freight</p>

<p>$373,509</p>

<p>$268,107</p>

<p>Telephone</p>

<p>$214,820</p>

<p>$192,721</p>

<p>Convention</p>
<p>Expense</p>

<p>$268,855</p>

<p>$268,855</p>

<p>See App. to Pet. for Cert. 35a-36a.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;How could any nonmember determine whether these numbers are even close to the mark without launching a legal challenge and retaining the services of attorneys and accountants?  Indeed, even with such services, it would be a laborious and difficult task to check these figures.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 26" name="FRopinion1.26" href="#FNopinion1.26">26</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Union respondent argues that challenging its chargeability determinations is not burdensome because the Union pays for the costs of arbitration, see Brief for Union Respondent 10-11, but objectors must still pay for the attorneys and experts needed to mount a serious challenge.  And the attorney's fees incurred in such a proceeding can be substantial.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Knox</em> v. <em>Chiang</em>, 2013 WL 2434606, *15 (ED Cal., June 5, 2013) (attorney's fees in <em>Knox</em> exceeded $1 million).  The Union respondent's suggestion that an objector could obtain adequate review without even showing up at an arbitration, see App. to Pet. for Cert. 40a-41a, is therefore farfetched.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">C</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Developments since <em>Abood</em>, both factual and legal, have also "eroded" the decision's "underpinnings" and left it an outlier among our First Amendment cases.  <em>United States</em> v. <em>Gaudin</em>, 515 U.&nbsp;S. 506, 521 (1995).</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">1</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Abood </em>pinned its result on the "unsupported empirical assumption" that "the principle of exclusive representation in the public sector is dependent on a union or agency shop."  <em>Harris</em>, 573 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (slip op., at 20); <em>Abood</em>, 431 U.&nbsp;S., at 220-222.  But, as already noted, experience has shown otherwise.  See <em>supra</em>, at 11-12.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;It is also significant that the Court decided <em>Abood</em> against a very different legal and economic backdrop.  Public-sector unionism was a relatively new phenomenon in 1977.  The first State to permit collective bargaining by government employees was Wisconsin in 1959, R. Kearney &amp; P. Mareschal, Labor Relations in the Public Sector 64 (5th ed. 2014), and public-sector union membership remained relatively low until a "spurt" in the late 1960's and early 1970's, shortly before <em>Abood</em> was decided, Freeman, Unionism Comes to the Public Sector, 24 J. Econ. Lit. 41, 45 (1986).  Since then, public-sector union membership has come to surpass private-sector union membership, even though there are nearly four times as many total private-sector employees as public-sector employees.  B. Hirsch &amp; D. Macpherson, Union Membership and Earnings Data Book 9-10, 12, 16 (2013 ed.).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;This ascendance of public-sector unions has been marked by a parallel increase in public spending.  In 1970, total state and local government expenditures amounted to $646 per capita in nominal terms, or about $4,000 per capita in 2014 dollars.  See Dept. of Commerce, Statistical Abstract of the United States: 1972, p. 419; CPI Inflation Calculator, BLS, http://data.bls.gov/cgi-bin/cpicalc.pl.  By 2014, that figure had ballooned to approximately $10,238 per capita.  ProQuest, Statistical Abstract of the United States: 2018, pp. 17, Table 14, 300, Table 469.  Not all that increase can be attributed to public-sector unions, of course, but the mounting costs of public-employee wages, benefits, and pensions undoubtedly played a substantial role.  We are told, for example, that Illinois' pension funds are underfunded by $129 billion as a result of generous public-employee retirement packages.  Brief for Jason R. Barclay et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae </em>9, 14.  Unsustainable collective-bargaining agreements have also been blamed for multiple municipal bankruptcies.  See Brief for State of Michigan et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 10-19.  These developments, and the political debate over public spending and debt they have spurred, have given collective-bargaining issues a political valence that <em>Abood</em> did not fully appreciate.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">2</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Abood</em> is also an "anomaly" in our First Amendment jurisprudence, as we recognized in <em>Harris </em>and <em>Knox</em>.  <em>Harris</em>, <em>supra</em>, at ___ (slip op., at 8); <em>Knox</em>, 567 U.&nbsp;S., at 311.  This is not an altogether new observation.  In <em>Abood </em>itself, Justice Powell faulted the Court for failing to perform the "&nbsp;'exacting scrutiny'&nbsp;" applied in other cases involving significant impingements on First Amendment rights.  431 U.&nbsp;S., at 259; see <em>id.</em>, at 259-260, and n.&nbsp;14.  Our later cases involving compelled speech and association have also employed exacting scrutiny, if not a more demanding standard.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Roberts</em>, 468 U.&nbsp;S., at 623; <em>United Foods</em>, 533 U.&nbsp;S., at 414.  And we have more recently refused, even in agency-fee cases, to extend <em>Abood </em>beyond circumstances where it directly controls.  See <em>Knox</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 314; <em>Harris</em>, <em>supra</em>, at ___-___ (slip op., at 28-29).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Abood </em>particularly sticks out when viewed against our   </p>
<p>cases holding that public employees generally may not be required to support a political party.  See <em>Elrod</em>, 427 U.&nbsp;S. 347; <em>Branti</em>, 445 U.&nbsp;S. 507; <em>Rutan</em>, 497 U.&nbsp;S. 62; <em>O'Hare Truck Service, Inc.</em> v. <em>City of Northlake</em>, 518 U.&nbsp;S. 712 (1996).  The Court reached that conclusion despite a "long tradition" of political patronage in government.  <em>Rutan</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 95 (Scalia<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>, J.,</small></span> dissenting); see also <em>Elrod</em>, 427 U.&nbsp;S., at 353 (plurality opinion); <em>id.</em>, at 377-378 (Powell, J., dissenting).  It is an odd feature of our First Amendment cases that political patronage has been deemed largely unconstitutional, while forced subsidization of union speech (which has no such pedigree) has been largely permitted.  As Justice Powell observed: "I am at a loss  to understand why the State's decision to adopt the agency shop in the public sector should be worthy of <em>greater</em> deference, when challenged on First Amendment grounds, than its decision to adhere to the <em>tradition</em> of political patronage."  <em>Abood</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 260, n.&nbsp;14 (opinion concurring in judgment) (citing <em>Elrod</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 376-380, 382-387 (Powell, J., dissenting); emphasis added).  We have no occasion here to reconsider our political patronage decisions, but Justice Powell's observation is sound as far as it goes.  By overruling <em>Abood</em>, we end the oddity of privileging compelled union support over compelled party support and bring a measure of greater coherence to our First Amendment law.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">D</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In some cases, reliance provides a strong reason for adhering to established law, see, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Hilton</em> v. <em>South Carolina Public Railways Comm'n</em>, 502 U.&nbsp;S. 197, 202-203 (1991), and this is the factor that is stressed most strongly by respondents, their <em>amici</em>, and the dissent.  They contend that collective-bargaining agreements now in effect were negotiated with agency fees in mind and that unions may have given up other benefits in exchange for provisions granting them such fees.  Tr. of Oral Arg. 67-68; see Brief for State Respondents 54; Brief for Union Respondent 50; <em>post</em>, at 22-26 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kagan, J.</small></span>, dissenting).  In this case, however, reliance does not carry decisive weight.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;For one thing, it would be unconscionable to permit free speech rights to be abridged in perpetuity in order to preserve contract provisions that will expire on their own in a few years' time.  "The fact that [public-sector unions] may view [agency fees] as an entitlement does not establish the sort of reliance interest that could outweigh the countervailing interest that [nonmembers] share in having their constitutional rights fully protected."  <em>Arizona</em> v. <em>Gant</em>, 556 U.&nbsp;S. 332, 349 (2009).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;For another, <em>Abood</em> does not provide "a clear or easily applicable standard, so arguments for reliance based on its clarity are misplaced."  <em>South Dakota</em> v. <em>Wayfair, Inc.</em>, <em>ante</em>, at<em> </em>20; see <em>supra</em>, at 38-41.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;This is especially so because public-sector unions have been on notice for years regarding this Court's misgivings about <em>Abood</em>.  In <em>Knox</em>, decided in 2012, we described <em>Abood</em> as a First Amendment "anomaly."  567 U.&nbsp;S., at 311.  Two years later in <em>Harris</em>, we were asked to overrule <em>Abood</em>, and while we found it unnecessary to take that step, we cataloged <em>Abood</em>'s many weaknesses.  In 2015, we granted a petition for certiorari asking us to review a decision that sustained an agency-fee arrangement under <em>Abood</em>.  <em>Friedrichs</em> v. <em>California Teachers Assn.</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S. ___.  After exhaustive briefing and argument on the question whether <em>Abood</em> should be overruled, we affirmed the decision below by an equally divided vote.  578 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2016) (<em>per curiam</em>).  During this period of time, any public-sector union seeking an agency-fee provision in a collective-bargaining agreement must have understood that the constitutionality of such a provision was uncertain.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;That is certainly true with respect to the collective-bargaining agreement in the present case.  That agreement initially ran from July 1, 2012, until June 30, 2015.  App. 331.  Since then, the agreement has been extended pursuant to a provision providing for automatic renewal for an additional year unless either party gives timely notice that it desires to amend or terminate the contract.  <em>Ibid</em>.  Thus, for the past three years, the Union could not have been confident about the continuation of the agency-fee arrangement for more than a year at a time.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Because public-sector collective-bargaining agreements are generally of rather short duration, a great many of those now in effect probably began or were renewed since <em>Knox</em> (2012) or <em>Harris</em> (2014).  But even if an agreement antedates those decisions, the union was able to protect itself if an agency-fee provision was essential to the overall bargain.  A union's attorneys undoubtedly understand that if one provision of a collective-bargaining agreement is found to be unlawful, the remaining provisions are likely to remain in effect.  See <em>NLRB</em> v. <em>Rockaway News Supply Co.</em>, 345 U.&nbsp;S. 71, 76-79 (1953); see also 8 R. Lord, Williston on Contracts §19:70 (4th ed. 2010).  Any union believing that an agency-fee provision was essential to its bargain could have insisted on a provision giving it greater protection.  The agreement in the present case, by contrast, provides expressly that the invalidation of any part of the agreement "shall not invalidate the remaining portions," which "shall remain in full force and effect."  App. 328.  Such severability clauses ensure that "entire contracts" are not "br[ought] down" by today's ruling.  <em>Post</em>, at 23, n.&nbsp;5 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kagan, J.</small></span>, dissenting).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In short, the uncertain status of <em>Abood</em>, the lack of clarity it provides, the short-term nature of collective-bargaining agreements, and the ability of unions to protect themselves if an agency-fee provision was crucial to its bargain all work to undermine the force of reliance as a factor supporting <em>Abood</em>.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 27" name="FRopinion1.27" href="#FNopinion1.27">27</a></sup></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">*  *  *</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;We recognize that the loss of payments from nonmembers may cause unions to experience unpleasant transition costs in the short term, and may require unions to make adjustments in order to attract and retain members.  But we must weigh these disadvantages against the considerable windfall that unions have received under <em>Abood </em>for the past 41 years.  It is hard to estimate how many billions of dollars have been taken from nonmembers and transferred to public-sector unions in violation of the First Amendment.  Those unconstitutional exactions cannot be allowed to continue indefinitely.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;All these reasons--that <em>Abood</em>'s proponents have abandoned its reasoning, that the precedent has proved unworkable, that it conflicts with other First Amendment decisions, and that subsequent developments have eroded its underpinnings--provide the "&nbsp;'special justification[s]'&nbsp;" for overruling <em>Abood</em>.  <em>Post</em>, at 19 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kagan, J.</small></span>, dissenting) (quoting <em>Kimble</em> v. <em>Marvel Entertainment, LLC</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2015) (slip op., at 8)).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 28" name="FRopinion1.28" href="#FNopinion1.28">28</a></sup></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">VII</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;For these reasons, States and public-sector unions may no longer extract agency fees from nonconsenting employees.  Under Illinois law, if a public-sector collective-bargaining agreement includes an agency-fee provision and the union certifies to the employer the amount of the fee, that amount is automatically deducted from the nonmember's wages.  §315/6(e).  No form of employee consent is required.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;This procedure violates the First Amendment and cannot continue.  Neither an agency fee nor any other payment to the union may be deducted from a nonmember's wages, nor may any other attempt be made to collect such a payment, unless the employee affirmatively consents to pay.  By agreeing to pay, nonmembers are waiving their First Amendment rights, and such a waiver cannot be presumed.  <em>Johnson</em> v. <em>Zerbst</em>, 304 U.&nbsp;S. 458, 464 (1938); see also <em>Knox</em>, 567 U. S., at 312-313.  Rather, to be effective, the waiver must be freely given and shown by "clear and compelling" evidence.  <em>Curtis Publishing Co.</em> v. <em>Butts</em>, 388 U.&nbsp;S. 130, 145 (1967) (plurality opinion); see also <em>College Savings Bank</em> v. <em>Florida Prepaid Postsecondary Ed. Expense Bd.</em>, 527 U.&nbsp;S. 666, 680-682 (1999).  Unless employees clearly and affirmatively consent before any money is taken from them, this standard cannot be met.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">*  *  *</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Abood </em>was wrongly decided and is now overruled.  The judgment of the United States Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit is reversed, and the case is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.</p>
<p align="RIGHT">It is so ordered.</p>
<p></p><hr>





<p align="CENTER"><span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor, J.</small></span>, dissenting</p>
<p align="CENTER"> 585 U.&nbsp;S. ____ (2018)</p>

<p align="CENTER">No. 16-1466</p>


<center><h2><p align="CENTER">MARK JANUS, PETITIONER <em>v.</em> AMERICAN FEDERATION OF STATE, COUNTY, AND MUNICIPAL EMPLOYEES, COUNCIL 31, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>et&nbsp;al.</small></span></p>
</h2></center><center><h4><p align="CENTER"><span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>on writ of certiorari to the united states court of appeals for the seventh circuit</small></span></p>
</h4></center><center><p align="CENTER">[June 27, 2018]</p>
</center><p></p><hr>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Sotomayor</small></span>, dissenting.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I join <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kagan'</small></span>s<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small> </small></span>dissent in full.  Although I joined the majority in <em>Sorrell</em> v. <em>IMS Health Inc.</em>, 564 U.&nbsp;S. 552 (2011), I disagree with the way that this Court has since interpreted and applied that opinion.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>National Institute of Family and Life Advocates</em> v. <em>Becerra</em>, <em>ante,</em>  p.<em> </em>___.  Having seen the troubling development in First Amendment jurisprudence over the years, both in this Court and in lower courts, I agree fully with <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kagan</small></span> that <em>Sorrell</em>--in the way it has been read by this Court--has allowed courts to "wiel[d] the First Amendment in .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. an aggressive way" just as the majority does today.  <em>Post, </em>at 27.</p>
<p></p><hr>




<p align="CENTER"><span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kagan, J.</small></span>, dissenting</p>
<p align="CENTER"> 585 U.&nbsp;S. ____ (2018)</p>


<p align="CENTER">No. 16-1466</p>


<center><h2><p align="CENTER">MARK JANUS, PETITIONER <em>v.</em> AMERICAN FEDERATION OF STATE, COUNTY, AND MUNICIPAL EMPLOYEES, COUNCIL 31, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>et&nbsp;al.</small></span></p>
</h2></center><center><h4><p align="CENTER"><span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>on writ of certiorari to the united states court of appeals for the seventh circuit</small></span></p>
</h4></center><center><p align="CENTER">[June 27, 2018]</p>
</center><p></p><hr>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kagan</small></span>, with whom J<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>ustice Ginsburg</small></span>, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Breyer, </small></span>and <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Sotomayor</small></span> join,  dissenting.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;For over 40 years, <em>Abood</em> v. <em>Detroit Bd. of Ed.</em>, 431 U.&nbsp;S. 209 (1977), struck a stable balance between public employees' First Amendment rights and government entities' interests in running their workforces as they thought proper.  Under that decision, a government entity could require public employees to pay a fair share of the cost that a union incurs when negotiating on their behalf over terms of employment.  But no part of that fair-share payment could go to any of the union's political or ideological activities.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;That holding fit comfortably with this Court's general framework for evaluating claims that a condition of public employment violates the First Amendment.  The Court's decisions have long made plain that government entities have substantial latitude to regulate their employees' speech--especially about terms of employment--in the interest of operating their workplaces effectively.  <em>Abood </em>allowed governments to do just that.  While protecting public employees' expression about non-workplace matters, the decision enabled a government to advance important managerial interests--by ensuring the presence of an exclusive employee representative to bargain with.  Far from an "anomaly," <em>ante,</em> at 7, the <em>Abood </em>regime was a paradigmatic example of how the government can regulate speech in its capacity as an employer.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Not any longer.  Today, the Court succeeds in its 6-year campaign to reverse <em>Abood</em>.  See <em>Friedrichs</em> v. <em>California Teachers Assn.</em>, 578 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2016) (<em>per curiam</em>); <em>Harris</em> v. <em>Quinn</em>, 573 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2014); <em>Knox</em> v. <em>Service Employees</em>, 567 U.&nbsp;S. 298 (2012).  Its decision will have large-scale consequences.  Public employee unions will lose a secure source of financial support.  State and local governments that thought fair-share provisions furthered their interests will need to find new ways of managing their workforces.  Across the country, the relationships of public employees and employers will alter in both predictable and wholly unexpected ways.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Rarely if ever has the Court overruled a decision--let alone one of this import--with so little regard for the usual principles of <em>stare decisis</em>.  There are no special justifications for reversing <em>Abood.</em>  It has proved workable.  No recent developments have eroded its underpinnings.  And it is deeply entrenched, in both the law and the real world.  More than 20 States have statutory schemes built on the decision.  Those laws underpin thousands of ongoing contracts involving millions of employees.  Reliance interests do not come any stronger than those surrounding <em>Abood</em>.  And likewise, judicial disruption does not get any greater than what the Court does today.  I respectfully dissent.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">I</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I begin with <em>Abood</em>, the 41-year-old precedent the majority overrules.  That case involved a union that had been certified as the exclusive representative of Detroit's public school teachers.  The union's collective-bargaining agreement with the city included an "agency shop" clause, which required teachers who had not joined the union to pay it "a service charge equal to the regular dues required of [u]nion members."  <em>Abood</em>, 431 U.&nbsp;S., at 212.  A group of non-union members sued over that clause, arguing that it violated the First Amendment.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In considering their challenge, the Court canvassed the purposes of the "agency shop" clause.  It was rooted, the Court understood, in the "principle of exclusive union representation"--a "central element" in "industrial relations" since the New Deal.  <em>Id.,</em> at 220.  Significant benefits, the Court explained, could derive from the "designation of a single [union] representative" for all similarly situated employees in a workplace.  <em>Ibid.  </em>In particular, such arrangements: "avoid[&nbsp;] the confusion that would result from attempting to enforce two or more agreements specifying  different terms and conditions of employment"; "prevent[&nbsp;] inter-union rivalries from creating dissension within the work force"; "free[&nbsp;] the employer from the possibility of facing conflicting demands from different unions"; and "permit[&nbsp;] the employer and a single union to reach agreements and settlements that are not subject to attack from rival labor organizations."  <em>Id.,</em> at 220-221.  As proof, the Court pointed to the example of exclusive-representation arrangements in the private-employment sphere: There, Congress had long thought that such schemes would promote "peaceful labor relations" and "labor stability."  <em>Id.,</em> at 219, 229.  A public employer like Detroit, the Court believed, could reasonably make the same calculation.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;But for an exclusive-bargaining arrangement to work, such an employer often thought, the union needed adequate funding.  Because the "designation of a union as exclusive representative carries with it great responsibilities," the Court reasoned, it inevitably also entails substantial costs.  <em>Id.,</em> at 221.  "The tasks of negotiating and administering a collective-bargaining agreement and representing the interests of employees in settling disputes and processing grievances are continuing and difficult ones."  <em>Ibid.</em>  Those activities, the Court noted, require the "expenditure of much time and money"--for example, payment for the "services of lawyers, expert negotiators, economists, and a research staff."  <em>Ibid.</em>  And there is no way to confine the union's services to union members alone (and thus to trim costs) because unions must by law fairly represent all employees in a given bargaining unit--union members and non-members alike.  See <em>ibid.</em></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;With all that in mind, the Court recognized why both a government entity and its union bargaining partner would gravitate toward an agency-fee clause.  Those fees, the Court reasoned, "distribute fairly the cost" of collective bargaining "among those who benefit"--that is, <em>all </em>employees in the work unit.  <em>Id.,</em> at 222.  And they "counteract[&nbsp;] the incentive that employees might otherwise have to become 'free riders.'&nbsp;"  <em>Ibid.</em>  In other words, an agency-fee provision prevents employees from reaping all the "benefits of union representation"--higher pay, a better retirement plan, and so forth--while leaving it to others to bear the costs.  <em>Ibid.  </em>To the Court, the upshot was clear: A government entity could reasonably conclude that such a clause was needed to maintain the kind of exclusive bargaining arrangement that would facilitate peaceful and stable labor relations.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;But the Court acknowledged as well the "First Amendment interests" of dissenting employees.  <em>Ibid.  </em>It recognized that some workers might oppose positions the union takes in collective bargaining, or even "unionism itself."  <em>Ibid.  </em>And still more, it understood that unions often advance "political and ideological" views outside the  collective-bargaining context--as when they "contribute to political candidates."  <em>Id.,</em> at 232, 234.  Employees might well object to the use of their money to support such "ideological causes."  <em>Id.,</em> at 235.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;So the Court struck a balance, which has governed this area ever since.  On the one hand, employees could be required to pay fees to support the union in "collective bargaining, contract administration, and grievance adjustment."  <em>Id.,</em> at 225-226.  There, the Court held, the "important government interests" in having a stably  funded bargaining partner justify "the impingement upon" public employees' expression.  <em>Id.,</em> at 225.  But on the other hand, employees could not be compelled to fund the union's political and ideological activities.  Outside the collective-bargaining sphere, the Court determined, an employee's First Amendment rights defeated any conflicting government interest.  See <em>id.,</em> at 234-235.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">II</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Unlike the majority, I see nothing "questionable" about <em>Abood</em>'s analysis.  <em>Ante,</em> at 7 (quoting <em>Harris</em>, 573 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (slip op., at 17)).  The decision's account of why some government entities have a strong interest in agency fees (now often called fair-share fees) is fundamentally sound.  And the balance <em>Abood </em>struck between public employers' interests and public employees' expression is right at home in First Amendment doctrine.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">A</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Abood</em>'s reasoning about governmental interests has three connected parts.  First, exclusive representation arrangements benefit some government entities because they can facilitate stable labor relations.  In particular, such arrangements eliminate the potential for inter-union conflict and streamline the process of negotiating terms of employment.  See 431 U.&nbsp;S., at 220-221. <em> </em>Second, the government may be unable to avail itself of those benefits unless the single union has a secure source of funding.  The various tasks involved in representing employees cost money; if the union doesn't have enough, it can't be an effective employee representative and bargaining partner.  See <em>id.</em>, at 221.  And third, agency fees are often needed to ensure such stable funding.  That is because without those fees, employees have every incentive to free ride on the union dues paid by others.  See <em>id.</em>, at 222.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The majority does not take issue with the first point.  See <em>ante,</em> at 33 (It is "not disputed that the State may require that a union serve as exclusive bargaining agent for its employees" in order to advance the State's "interests as an employer").  The majority claims that the second point never appears in <em>Abood</em>, but is willing to assume it for the sake of argument.  See <em>ante,</em> at 31-32; but see <em>Abood</em>, 431 U.&nbsp;S., at 221 (The tasks of an exclusive representative "often entail expenditure of much time and money").  So the majority stakes everything on the third point--the conclusion that maintaining an effective system of exclusive representation often entails agency fees.  <em>Ante</em>, at 12 (It "is simply not true" that exclusive representation and agency fees are "inextricably linked"); see <em>ante, </em>at 14.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;But basic economic theory shows why a government would think that agency fees are necessary for exclusive representation to work.  What ties the two together, as <em>Abood </em>recognized, is the likelihood of free-riding when fees are absent.  Remember that once a union achieves  exclusive-representation status, the law compels it to fairly represent all workers in the bargaining unit, whether or not they join or contribute to the union.  See <em>supra,</em> at 4.  Because of that legal duty, the union cannot give special advantages to its own members.  And that in turn creates a collective action problem of nightmarish proportions.  Everyone--not just those who oppose the union, but also those who back it--has an economic incentive to withhold dues; only altruism or loyalty--as <em>against</em> financial self-interest--can explain why an employee would pay the union for its services.  And so emerged <em>Abood</em>'s rule allowing fair-share agreements: That rule ensured that a union would receive sufficient funds, despite its legally imposed disability, to effectively carry out its duties as exclusive representative of the government's employees.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The majority's initial response to this reasoning is  simply to dismiss it.  "[F]ree rider arguments," the majority pronounces, "are generally insufficient to overcome First Amendment objections."  <em>Ante,</em> at 13 (quoting <em>Knox</em>, 567 U.&nbsp;S., at 311).  "To hold otherwise," it continues, "would have startling consequences" because "[m]any private groups speak out" in ways that will "benefit[&nbsp;] nonmembers."  <em>Ante,</em> at 13<em>.</em>  But that disregards the defining characteristic of <em>this </em>free-rider argument--that unions, unlike those many other private groups, must serve members and non-members alike.  Groups advocating for "senior citizens or veterans" (to use the majority's examples) have no legal duty to provide benefits to all those individuals: They can spur people to pay dues by conferring all kinds of special advantages on their dues-paying members.  Unions are--by law--in a different position, as this Court has long recognized.  See, <em>e.g., Machinists</em> v. <em>Street</em>, 367 U.&nbsp;S. 740, 762 (1961).  Justice Scalia, responding to the same argument as the majority's, may have put the point best.  In a way that is true of no other private group, the "law <em>requires </em>the union to carry" non-members--"indeed, requires the union to <em>go out of its way </em>to benefit [them], even at the expense of its other interests."  <em>Lehnert</em> v. <em>Ferris Faculty Assn.</em>, 500 U.&nbsp;S. 507, 556 (1991) (opinion concurring in part and dissenting in part).  That special feature was what justified <em>Abood</em>: "Where the state imposes upon the union a duty to deliver services, it may permit the union to demand reimbursement for them."  500 U.&nbsp;S., at 556.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The majority's fallback argument purports to respond to the distinctive position of unions, but still misses <em>Abood</em>'s economic insight.  Here, the majority delivers a four-page exegesis on why unions will seek to serve as an exclusive bargaining representative even "if they are not given agency fees."  <em>Ante,</em> at 14; see <em>ante,</em> at 14-17.  The gist of the account is that "designation as the exclusive representative confers many benefits," which outweigh the costs of providing services to non-members.  <em>Ante,</em> at 15.  But that response avoids the key question, which is whether unions without agency fees will be <em>able to</em> (not whether they will <em>want to</em>)<em> </em>carry on as an effective exclusive representative.  And as to that question, the majority again fails to reckon with how economically rational actors behave--in public as well as private workplaces.  Without a fair-share agreement, the class of union non-members spirals upward.  Employees (including those who love the union) realize that they can get the same benefits even if they let their memberships expire.  And as more and more stop paying dues, those left must take up the financial slack (and anyway, begin to feel like suckers)--so they too quit the union.  See Ichniowski &amp; Zax, Right-to-Work Laws, Free Riders, and Unionization in the Local Public Sector, 9 J. Labor Economics 255, 257 (1991).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 1" name="FRopinion1.1" href="#FNopinion1.1">1</a></sup>  And when the vicious cycle finally ends, chances are that the union will lack the resources to effectively perform the responsibilities of an exclusive representative--or, in the worst case, to perform them at all.  The result is to frustrate the interests of every government entity that thinks a strong exclusive-representation scheme will promote stable labor relations.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Of course, not all public employers will share that view.  Some would rather not bargain with an exclusive representative.  Others would prefer that representative to be poorly funded--to serve more as a front than an effectual bargaining partner.  But as reflected in the number of fair-share statutes and contracts across the Nation, see <em>supra,</em> at 2, many government entities think that effective exclusive representation makes for good labor relations--and recognize, just as <em>Abood </em>did, that representation of that kind often depends on agency fees.  See, <em>e.g., Harris</em>, 573 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (slip op., at 24) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kagan, J., </small></span>dissenting) (describing why Illinois thought that bargaining with an adequately funded exclusive representative of in-home caregivers would enable the State to better serve its disabled citizens).  <em>Abood </em>respected that state interest; today's majority fails even to understand it.  Little wonder that the majority's First Amendment analysis, which involves assessing the government's reasons for imposing agency fees, also comes up short.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">B</p>
</h2><h3><p align="CENTER">1</p>
</h3><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In many cases over many decades, this Court has addressed how the First Amendment applies when the government, acting not as sovereign but as employer, limits its workers' speech.  Those decisions have granted substantial latitude to the government, in recognition of its significant interests in managing its workforce so as to best serve the public.  <em>Abood </em>fit neatly with that caselaw, in both reasoning and result.  Indeed, its reversal today creates a significant anomaly--an exception, applying to union fees alone, from the usual rules governing public employees' speech.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"Time and again our cases have recognized that the Government has a much freer hand" in dealing with its employees than with "citizens at large."  <em>NASA </em>v. <em>Nelson</em>, 562 U.&nbsp;S. 134, 148 (2011) (internal quotation marks omitted).  The government, we have stated, needs to run "as effectively and efficiently as possible."  <em>Engquist </em>v. <em>Oregon Dept. of Agriculture</em>, 553 U.&nbsp;S. 591, 598 (2008) (internal quotation marks omitted).  That means it must be able, much as a private employer is, to manage its workforce as it thinks fit.  A public employee thus must submit to "certain limitations on his or her freedom."  <em>Garcetti</em> v. <em>Ceballos</em>, 547 U.&nbsp;S. 410, 418 (2006).  Government workers, of course, do not wholly "lose their constitutional rights when they accept their positions."  <em>Engquist</em>, 553 U.&nbsp;S., at 600.  But under our precedent, their rights often yield when weighed "against the realities of the employment context."  <em>Ibid.</em>  If it were otherwise--if every employment decision were to "bec[o]me a constitutional matter"--"the Government could not function."  <em>NASA</em>, 562 U.&nbsp;S., at 149 (internal quotation marks omitted).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Those principles apply with full force when public  employees' expressive rights are at issue.  As we have explained: "Government employers, like private employers, need a significant degree of control over their employees' words" in order to "efficient[ly] provi[de] public services."  <em>Garcetti</em>, 547 U.&nbsp;S., at 418.  Again, significant control does not mean absolute authority.  In particular, the Court has guarded against government efforts to "leverage the employment relationship" to shut down its employees' speech as private citizens.  <em>Id.,</em> at 419.  But when the government imposes speech restrictions relating to workplace operations, of the kind a private employer also would, the Court reliably upholds them.  See, <em>e.g., id.</em>, at 426; <em>Connick</em> v. <em>Myers</em>, 461 U.&nbsp;S. 138, 154 (1983).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In striking the proper balance between employee speech rights and managerial interests, the Court has long applied a test originating in <em>Pickering</em> v. <em>Board of Ed. of Township High School Dist. 205, Will Cty.</em>, 391 U.&nbsp;S. 563 (1968).  That case arose out of an individual employment action: the firing of a public school teacher.  As we later described the <em>Pickering </em>inquiry<em>, </em>the Court first asks whether the employee "spoke as a citizen on a matter of public concern."  <em>Garcetti</em>, 547 U.&nbsp;S., at 418.  If she did not--but rather spoke as an employee on a workplace matter--she has no "possibility of a First Amendment claim": A public employer can curtail her speech just as a private one could.  <em>Ibid.  </em>But if she did speak as a citizen on a public matter, the public employer must demonstrate "an adequate justification for treating the employee differently from any other member of the general public."  <em>Ibid.  </em>The government, that is, needs to show that legitimate workplace interests lay behind the speech regulation.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Abood </em>coheres with that framework.  The point here is not, as the majority suggests, that <em>Abood </em>is an overt, one-to-one "application of <em>Pickering</em>."  <em>Ante,</em> at 26.  It is not.  <em>Abood </em>related to a municipality's labor policy, and so the Court looked to prior cases about unions, not to <em>Pickering</em>'s analysis of an employee's dismissal.  (And truth be told, <em>Pickering </em>was not at that time much to look at: What the Court now thinks of as the two-step <em>Pickering </em>test, as the majority's own citations show, really emerged from <em>Garcetti </em>and <em>Connick</em>--two cases post-dating <em>Abood</em>.  See <em>ante, </em>at 22.)<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 2" name="FRopinion1.2" href="#FNopinion1.2">2</a></sup>  But <em>Abood </em>and <em>Pickering </em>raised variants of the same basic issue: the extent of the government's authority to make employment decisions affecting expression.  And in both, the Court struck the same basic balance, enabling the government to curb speech when--but only when--the regulation was designed to protect its managerial interests.  Consider the parallels:</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Like <em>Pickering</em>,<em> Abood </em>drew the constitutional line by analyzing the connection between the government's managerial interests and different kinds of expression.  The Court first discussed the use of agency fees to subsidize the speech involved in "collective bargaining, contract administration, and grievance adjustment."  431 U.&nbsp;S., at 225-226.  It understood that expression (really, who would not?) as intimately tied to the workplace and employment relationship.  The speech was about "working conditions, pay, discipline, promotions, leave, vacations, and terminations," <em>Borough of Duryea</em> v. <em>Guarnieri</em>, 564 U.&nbsp;S. 379, 391 (2011); the speech occurred (almost always) in the workplace; and the speech was directed (at least mainly) to the employer.  As noted earlier, <em>Abood</em> described the managerial interests of employers in channeling all that speech through a single union.  See 431 U.&nbsp;S., at 220-222, 224-226; <em>supra,</em> at 3.  And so <em>Abood </em>allowed the government to mandate fees for collective bargaining--just as <em>Pickering </em>permits the government to regulate employees' speech on similar workplace matters.  But still, <em>Abood </em>realized that compulsion could go too far.  The Court barred the use of fees for union speech supporting political candidates or "ideological causes."  431 U.&nbsp;S., at 235.  That speech, it understood, was "unrelated to [the union's] duties as exclusive bargaining representative," but instead was directed at the broader public sphere.  <em>Id.,</em> at 234.  And for that reason, the Court saw no legitimate managerial interests in compelling its subsidization.  The employees' First Amendment claims would thus prevail--as, again, they would have under <em>Pickering</em>.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Abood </em>thus dovetailed with the Court's usual attitude in First Amendment cases toward the regulation of public employees' speech.  That attitude is one of respect--even solicitude--for the government's prerogatives as an employer.  So long as the government is acting as an employer--rather than exploiting the employment relationship for other ends--it has a wide berth, comparable to that of a private employer.  And when the regulated expression concerns the terms and conditions of employment--the very stuff of the employment relationship--the government really cannot lose.  There, managerial interests are obvious and strong.  And so government employees are .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. just employees, even though they work for the government.  Except that today the government does lose, in a first for the law.  Now, the government can constitutionally adopt all policies regulating core workplace speech in pursuit of managerial goals--save this single one.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">2</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The majority claims it is not making a special and unjustified exception.  It offers two main reasons for declining to apply here our usual deferential approach, as exemplified in <em>Pickering</em>, to the regulation of public employee speech.  First, the majority says, this case involves a "blanket" policy rather than an individualized employment decision, so <em>Pickering </em>is a "painful fit."  <em>Ante, </em>at 23.  Second, the majority asserts, the regulation here involves compelling rather than restricting speech, so the pain gets sharper still.  See <em>ante</em>, at 24-25.<em><strong>  </strong></em>And finally, the majority claims that even under the solicitous <em>Pickering </em>standard, the government should lose, because the speech here involves a matter of public concern and the government's managerial interests do not justify its regulation.  See <em>ante,</em> at 27-31.  The majority goes wrong at every turn.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;First, this Court has applied the same basic approach whether a public employee challenges a general policy or an individualized decision.  Even the majority must concede that "we have sometimes looked to <em>Pickering </em>in considering general rules that affect broad categories of employees."  <em>Ante,</em> at 23.  In fact, the majority cannot come up with any case in which we have <em>not </em>done so.  All it can muster is one case in which <em>while </em>applying the <em>Pickering </em>test<em> </em>to a broad rule--barring any federal employee from accepting any payment for any speech or article on any topic--the Court noted that the policy's breadth would count against the government at the test's second step.  See <em>United States</em> v. <em>Treasury Employees</em>, 513 U.&nbsp;S. 454 (1995).  Which is completely predictable.  The inquiry at that stage, after all, is whether the government has an employment-related interest in going however far it has gone--and in <em>Treasury Employees</em>, the government had indeed gone far.  (The Court ultimately struck down the rule because it applied to speech in which the government had no identifiable managerial interest.  See <em>id.,</em> at 470, 477.)  Nothing in <em>Treasury Employees </em>suggests that the Court defers only to ad&nbsp;hoc actions, and not to general rules, about public employee speech.  That would be a perverse regime, given the greater regularity of rulemaking and the lesser danger of its abuse.  So I would wager a small fortune that the next time a general rule governing public employee speech comes before us, we will dust off <em>Pickering</em>.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Second, the majority's distinction between compelling and restricting speech also lacks force.  The majority posits that compelling speech always works a greater injury, and so always requires a greater justification.  See <em>ante,</em> at 8.  But the only case the majority cites for that reading of our precedent is possibly (thankfully) the most exceptional in our First Amendment annals: It involved the state forcing children to swear an oath contrary to their religious beliefs.  See <em>ibid.</em> (quoting <em>West Virginia Bd. of Ed.</em> v. <em>Barnette</em>, 319 U.&nbsp;S. 624 (1943)).  Regulations challenged as compelling expression do not usually look anything like that--and for that reason, the standard First Amendment rule is that the "difference between compelled speech and compelled silence" is "without constitutional significance."  <em>Riley</em> v. <em>National Federation of Blind of N. C., Inc.</em>, 487 U.&nbsp;S. 781, 796 (1988); see <em>Wooley</em> v. <em>Maynard</em>, 430 U.&nbsp;S. 705, 714 (1977) (referring to "[t]he right to speak and the right to refrain from speaking" as "complementary components" of the First Amendment).  And if anything, the First Amendment scales tip the opposite way when (as here) the government is not compelling actual speech, but instead compelling a subsidy that others will use for expression.  See Brief for Eugene Volokh et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 4-5 (offering many examples to show that the First Amendment "simply do[es] not guarantee that one's hard-earned dollars will never be spent on speech one disapproves of").<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 3" name="FRopinion1.3" href="#FNopinion1.3">3</a></sup>  So when a government mandates a speech subsidy from a public employee--here, we might think of it as levying a tax to support collective bargaining--it should get at least as much deference as when it restricts the employee's speech.  As this case shows, the former may advance a managerial interest as well as the latter--in which case the government's "freer hand" in dealing with its employees should apply with equal (if not greater) force.  <em>NASA</em>, 562 U.&nbsp;S., at 148.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Third and finally, the majority errs in thinking that under the usual deferential approach, the government should lose this case.  The majority mainly argues here that, at <em>Pickering</em>'s first step,<em> </em>"union speech in collective bargaining" is a "matter of great public concern" because it "affect[s] how public money is spent" and addresses "other important matters" like teacher merit pay or tenure.  <em>Ante,</em> at 27, 29 (internal quotation marks omitted).  But to start, the majority misunderstands the threshold inquiry set out in <em>Pickering </em>and later cases.  The question is not, as the majority seems to think, whether the public is, or should be, interested in a government employee's speech.  Instead, the question is whether that speech is about and directed to the workplace--as contrasted with the broader public square.  <em>Treasury Employees</em> offers the Court's fullest explanation.  The Court held there that the government's policy prevented employees from speaking as "citizen[s]" on "matters of public concern."  513 U.&nbsp;S., at 466 (quoting <em>Pickering</em>, 391 U.&nbsp;S., at 568).  Why?  Because the speeches and articles "were addressed to a public audience, were made outside the workplace, and involved content largely unrelated to their Government employment."  513 U.&nbsp;S., at 466; see <em>id.,</em> at 465, 470 (repeating that analysis twice more).  The Court could not have cared less whether the speech at issue was "important."  <em>Ante,</em> at 29.  It instead asked whether the speech was truly <em>of</em> the workplace--addressed <em>to</em> it, made <em>in</em> it, and (most of all) <em>about</em> it.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Consistent with that focus, speech about the terms and conditions of employment--the essential stuff of collective bargaining--has never survived <em>Pickering</em>'s first step.  This Court has rejected all attempts by employees to make a "federal constitutional issue" out of basic "employment matters, including working conditions, pay, discipline, promotions, leave, vacations, and terminations."  <em>Guarnieri</em>, 564 U.&nbsp;S., at 391; see <em>Board of Comm'rs, Wabaunsee </em> <em>Cty.</em> v. <em>Umbehr</em>, 518 U.&nbsp;S. 668, 675 (1996) (stating that public employees' "speech on merely private employment matters is unprotected").  For that reason, even the Justices who originally objected to <em>Abood </em>conceded that the use of agency fees for bargaining on "economic issues" like "salaries and pension benefits" would not raise significant First Amendment questions.  431 U.&nbsp;S., at 263, n.&nbsp;16 (Powell, J., concurring in judgment).  Of course, most of those issues have budgetary consequences: They "affect[&nbsp;] how public money is spent."  <em>Ante,</em> at 29.  And some raise important non-budgetary disputes; teacher merit pay is a good example, see <em>ante,</em> at 30.  But arguing about the terms of employment is still arguing about the terms of employment: The workplace remains both the context and the subject matter of the expression.  If all that speech really counted as "of public concern," as the majority suggests, the mass of public employees' complaints (about pay and benefits and workplace policy and such) <em>would </em>become "federal constitutional issue[s]."  <em>Guarnieri</em>, 564 U.&nbsp;S., at 391.  And contrary to decades' worth of precedent, government employers would then have far less control over their workforces than private employers do.  See <em>supra,</em> at 9-11.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Consider an analogy, not involving union fees: Suppose a government entity disciplines a group of (non-unionized) employees for agitating for a better health plan at various inopportune times and places.  The better health plan will of course drive up public spending; so according to the majority's analysis, the employees' speech satisfies <em>Pickering</em>'s "public concern" test.  Or similarly, suppose a public employer penalizes a group of (non-unionized) teachers who protest merit pay in the school cafeteria.  Once again, the majority's logic runs, the speech is of "public concern," so the employees have a plausible First Amendment claim.  (And indeed, the majority appears to concede as much, by asserting that the results in these hypotheticals should turn on various "factual detail[s]" relevant to the interest balancing that occurs at the <em>Pickering </em>test's<em> second</em> step.  <em>Ante,</em> at 32, n.&nbsp;23.)  But in fact, this Court has always understood such cases to end at <em>Pickering</em>'s <em>first</em> step: If an employee's speech is about, in, and directed to the workplace, she has no "possibility of a First Amendment claim."  <em>Garcetti</em>, 547 U.&nbsp;S., at 418; see <em>supra,</em> at 11.  So take your pick.  Either the majority is exposing government entities across the country to increased First Amendment litigation and liability--and thus preventing them from regulating their workforces as private employers could.  Or else, when actual cases of this kind come around, we will discover that today's majority has crafted a "unions only" carve-out to our employee-speech law.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;What's more, the government should prevail even if the speech involved in collective bargaining satisfies <em>Pickering</em>'s first part.  Recall that the next question is whether the government has shown "an adequate justification for treating the employee differently from any other member of the general public."  <em>Garcetti</em>, 547 U.&nbsp;S., at 418; <em>supra,</em> at 11.  That inquiry is itself famously respectful of government interests.  This Court has reversed the government only when it has tried to "leverage the employment relationship" to achieve an outcome unrelated to the workplace's "effective functioning."  <em>Garcetti</em>, 547 U.&nbsp;S., at 419; <em>Rankin</em> v. <em>McPherson</em>, 483 U.&nbsp;S. 378, 388 (1987).  Nothing like that is true here.  As <em>Abood </em>described, many government entities have found agency fees the best way to ensure a stable and productive relationship with an exclusive bargaining agent.  See 431 U.&nbsp;S., at 220-221, 224-226; <em>supra,</em> at 3-4.  And here, Illinois and many governmental <em>amici</em> have explained again how agency fees advance their workplace goals.  See Brief for State Respondents 12, 36; Brief for Governor Tom Wolf et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 21-33.  In no other employee-speech case has this Court dismissed such work-related interests, as the majority does here.  See <em>supra,</em> at 6-9 (discussing the majority's refusal to engage with the logic of the State's position).  Time and again, the Court has instead respected and acceded to those interests--just as <em>Abood </em>did.    </p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The key point about <em>Abood </em>is that it fit naturally with this Court's consistent teaching about the permissibility of regulating public employees' speech.  The Court allows a government entity to regulate that expression in aid of managing its workforce to effectively provide public services.  That is just what a government aims to do when it enforces a fair-share agreement.  And so, the key point about today's decision is that it creates an unjustified hole in the law, applicable to union fees alone.  This case is <em>sui generis </em>among those addressing public employee speech--and will almost surely remain so.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">III</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;But the worse part of today's opinion is where the majority subverts all known principles of <em>stare decisis</em>.  The majority makes plain, in the first 33 pages of its decision, that it believes <em>Abood </em>was wrong.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 4" name="FRopinion1.4" href="#FNopinion1.4">4</a></sup>  But even if that were true (which it is not), it is not enough.  "Respecting <em>stare decisis</em> means sticking to some wrong decisions."  <em>Kimble</em> v. <em>Marvel Entertainment, LLC</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2015) (slip op., at 7).  Any departure from settled precedent (so the Court has often stated) demands a "special justification--over and above the belief that the precedent was wrongly decided."  <em>Id.,</em> at ___ (slip op., at 8) (internal quotation marks omitted); see, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Arizona</em> v. <em>Rumsey</em>, 467 U.&nbsp;S. 203, 212 (1984).  And the majority does not have anything close.  To the contrary: all that is "special" in this case--especially the massive reliance interests at stake--demands retaining <em>Abood</em>, beyond even the normal  precedent.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Consider first why these principles about precedent are so important.  <em>Stare decisis</em>--"the idea that today's Court should stand by yesterday's decisions"--is "a foundation stone of the rule of law."  <em>Kimble, </em>576 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (slip op., at 7) (quoting<em> Michigan</em> v. <em>Bay Mills Indian Community</em>, 572 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2014) (slip op., at 15)).  It "promotes the evenhanded, predictable, and consistent development" of legal doctrine.  <em>Payne</em> v. <em>Tennessee</em>, 501 U.&nbsp;S. 808, 827 (1991).  It fosters respect for and reliance on judicial decisions.  See <em>ibid.  </em>And it "contributes to the actual and perceived integrity of the judicial process," <em>ibid.</em>, by ensuring that decisions are "founded in the law rather than in the proclivities of individuals," <em>Vasquez</em> v. <em>Hillery</em>, 474 U.&nbsp;S. 254, 265 (1986).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;And <em>Abood </em>is not just any precedent: It is embedded in the law (not to mention, as I'll later address, in the world) in a way not many decisions are.  Over four decades, this Court has cited <em>Abood </em>favorably many times, and has affirmed and applied its central distinction between the costs of collective bargaining (which the government can charge to all employees) and those of political activities (which it cannot).  See, <em>e.g., Locke</em> v. <em>Karass</em>, 555 U.&nbsp;S. 207, 213-214 (2009); <em>Lehnert</em>, 500 U.&nbsp;S., at 519; <em>Teachers</em> v. <em>Hudson</em>, 475 U.&nbsp;S. 292, 301-302 (1986); <em>Ellis</em> v. <em>Railway Clerks</em>, 466 U.&nbsp;S. 435, 455-457 (1984).  Reviewing those decisions not a decade ago, this Court--unanimously--called the <em>Abood </em>rule "a general First Amendment principle."  <em>Locke</em>, 555 U.&nbsp;S., at 213.  And indeed, the Court has relied on that rule when deciding cases involving compelled speech subsidies outside the labor sphere--cases today's decision does not question.  See, <em>e.g., Keller</em> v. <em>State Bar of Cal.</em>, 496 U.&nbsp;S. 1, 9-17 (1990) (state bar fees); <em>Board of Regents of Univ. of Wis. System</em> v. <em>Southworth</em>, 529 U.&nbsp;S. 217, 230-232 (2000) (public university student fees); <em>Glickman</em> v. <em>Wileman Brothers &amp; Elliott, Inc.</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S. 457, 471-473 (1997) (commercial advertising assessments); see also n.&nbsp;3, <em>supra</em>.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Ignoring our repeated validation of <em>Abood</em>, the majority claims it has become "an outlier among our First Amendment cases."  <em>Ante,</em> at 42.  That claim fails most spectacularly for reasons already discussed: <em>Abood </em>coheres with the <em>Pickering </em>approach to reviewing regulation of public employees' speech.  See <em>supra,</em> at 11-13.  Needing to stretch further, the majority suggests that <em>Abood </em>conflicts with "our political patronage decisions."  <em>Ante,</em> at 44.  But in fact those decisions strike a balance much like <em>Abood</em>'s.  On the one hand, the Court has enabled governments to compel policymakers to support a political party, because that requirement (like fees for collective bargaining) can reasonably be thought to advance the interest in workplace effectiveness.  See <em>Elrod</em> v. <em>Burns</em>, 427 U.&nbsp;S. 347, 366-367 (1976); <em>Branti</em> v. <em>Finkel</em>, 445 U.&nbsp;S. 507, 517 (1980).  On the other hand, the Court has barred governments from extending that rule to non-policymaking employees because that application (like fees for political campaigns) can't be thought to promote that interest, see <em>Elrod,</em> 427 U.&nbsp;S., at 366; the government is instead trying to "leverage the employment relationship" to achieve other goals, <em>Garcetti</em>, 547 U.&nbsp;S., at 419.
