So all that the majority has left is <em>Knox </em>and <em>Harris</em>.  See <em>ante,</em> at 43.  Dicta in those recent decisions indeed began the assault on <em>Abood </em>that has culminated today.  But neither actually addressed the extent to which a public employer may regulate its own employees' speech.  Relying on them is bootstrapping--and mocking <em>stare decisis</em>.  Don't like a decision?  Just throw some gratuitous criticisms into a couple of opinions and a few years later point to them as "special justifications."</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The majority is likewise wrong to invoke "workability" as a reason for overruling <em>Abood</em>.  <em>Ante,</em> at 38.  Does <em>Abood </em>require drawing a line?  Yes, between a union's collective-bargaining activities and its political activities.  Is that line perfectly and pristinely "precis[e]," as the majority demands?  <em>Ante,</em> at 38.  Well, not quite that--but as exercises of constitutional linedrawing go, <em>Abood </em>stands well above average.  In the 40 years since <em>Abood</em>, this Court has had to resolve only a handful of cases raising questions about the distinction.  To my knowledge, the circuit courts are not divided on any classification issue; neither are they issuing distress signals of the kind that sometimes prompt the Court to reverse a decision.  See, <em>e.g., Johnson </em>v. <em>United States, </em>576 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2015) (overruling precedent because of frequent splits and mass confusion).  And that tranquility is unsurprising: There may be some gray areas (there always are), but in the mine run of cases, everyone knows the difference between politicking and collective bargaining.  The majority cites some disagreement in two of the classification cases this Court decided--as if non-unanimity among Justices were something startling.  And it notes that a dissenter in one of those cases called the Court's approach "malleable" and "not principled," <em>ante,</em> at 39--as though those weren't stock terms in dissenting vocabulary.  See, <em>e.g., Murr</em> v. <em>Wisconsin</em>, 582 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2017) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Roberts, C.&nbsp;J.,</small></span> dissenting) (slip op., at 2); <em>Dietz</em> v. <em>Bouldin</em>, 579 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2016) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas, J.</small></span>, dissenting) (slip op., at 1); <em>Alabama Legislative Black Caucus</em> v. <em>Alabama</em>, 575 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2015) (slip op., at 13) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Scalia, J., </small></span>dissenting).  As I wrote in <em>Harris </em>a few Terms ago: "If the kind of hand-wringing about blurry lines that the majority offers were enough to justify breaking with precedent, we might have to discard whole volumes of the U.&nbsp;S. Reports."  573 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (slip op., at 15).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;And in any event, one <em>stare decisis</em> factor--reliance--dominates all others here and demands keeping <em>Abood</em>.  <em>Stare decisis</em>, this Court has held, "has added force when the legislature, in the public sphere, and citizens, in the private realm, have acted in reliance on a previous decision."  <em>Hilton</em> v. <em>South Carolina Public Railways Comm'n</em>, 502 U.&nbsp;S. 197, 202 (1991).  That is because overruling a decision would then "require an extensive legislative response" or "dislodge settled rights and expectations."  <em>Ibid.  </em>Both will happen here: The Court today wreaks havoc on entrenched legislative and contractual  arrangements.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Over 20 States have by now enacted statutes authorizing fair-share provisions.  To be precise, 22 States, the District of Columbia, and Puerto Rico--plus another two States for police and firefighter unions.  Many of those States have multiple statutory provisions, with variations for different categories of public employees.  See, <em>e.g., </em>Brief for State of California as <em>Amicus Curiae</em> 24-25.  Every one of them will now need to come up with new ways--elaborated in new statutes--to structure relations between government employers and their workers.  The majority responds, in a footnote no less, that this is of no proper concern to the Court.  See <em>ante,</em> at 47, n. 27.  But in fact, we have weighed heavily against "abandon[ing] our settled jurisprudence" that "[s]tate legislatures have relied upon" it and would have to "reexamine [and amend] their statutes" if it were overruled.  <em>Allied-Signal, Inc.</em> v. <em>Director, Div. of Taxation</em>, 504 U.&nbsp;S. 768, 785 (1992); <em>Hilton</em>, 502 U.&nbsp;S., at 203.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Still more, thousands of current contracts covering millions of workers provide for agency fees.  Usually, this Court recognizes that "[c]onsiderations in favor of <em>stare decisis</em> are at their acme in cases involving property and contract rights."  <em>Payne</em>, 501 U.&nbsp;S., at 828.  Not today.  The majority undoes bargains reached all over the country.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 5" name="FRopinion1.5" href="#FNopinion1.5">5</a></sup>  It prevents the parties from fulfilling other commitments they have made based on those agreements.  It forces the parties--immediately--to renegotiate once-settled terms and create new tradeoffs.  It does so knowing that many of the parties will have to revise (or redo) multiple contracts simultaneously.  (New York City, for example, has agreed to agency fees in 144 contracts with 97 public-sector unions.  See Brief for New York City Municipal Labor Committee as <em>Amicus Curiae</em> 4.)  It does so knowing that those renegotiations will occur in an environment of legal uncertainty, as state governments scramble to enact new labor legislation.  See <em>supra,</em> at 23.  It does so with no real clue of what will happen next--of how its action will alter public-sector labor relations.  It does so even though the government services affected--policing, firefighting, teaching, transportation, sanitation (and more)--affect the quality of life of tens of millions of Americans.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The majority asserts that no one should care much because the canceled agreements are "of rather short duration" and would "expire on their own in a few years' time."  <em>Ante,</em> at 45, 46.  But to begin with, that response ignores the substantial time and effort that state legislatures will have to devote to revamping their statutory schemes.  See <em>supra,</em> at 23.  And anyway, it misunderstands the nature of contract negotiations when the parties have a continuing relationship.  The parties, in renewing an old collective-bargaining agreement, don't start on an empty page.  Instead, various "long-settled" terms--like fair-share provisions--are taken as a given.  Brief for Governor Tom Wolf et&nbsp;al. 11; see Brief for New York City Sergeants Benevolent Assn. as <em>Amicus Curiae</em> 18.  So the majority's ruling does more than advance by a few years a future renegotiation (though even that would be significant).  In most cases, it commands new bargaining over how to replace a term that the parties never expected to change.  And not just new bargaining; given the interests at stake, complicated and possibly contentious bargaining as well.  See Brief for Governor Tom Wolf et&nbsp;al. 11.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 6" name="FRopinion1.6" href="#FNopinion1.6">6</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The majority, though, offers another reason for not worrying about reliance: The parties, it says, "have been on notice for years regarding this Court's misgivings about <em>Abood.</em>"  <em>Ante,</em> at 45.  Here, the majority proudly lays claim to its 6-year crusade to ban agency fees.  In <em>Knox</em>, the majority relates, it described <em>Abood </em>as an "anomaly."  <em>Ante,</em> at 45 (quoting 567 U.&nbsp;S., at 311).  Then, in <em>Harris</em>, it "cataloged <em>Abood</em>'s many weaknesses."  <em>Ante,</em> at 45.   Finally, in <em>Friedrichs</em>, "we granted a petition for certiorari asking us to" reverse <em>Abood</em>, but found ourselves equally divided.  <em>Ante,</em> at 45.<em>  </em>"During this period of time," the majority concludes, public-sector unions "must have understood that the constitutionality of [an agency-fee] provision was uncertain."  <em>Ibid</em>.  And so, says the majority, they should have structured their affairs accordingly.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;But that argument reflects a radically wrong understanding of how <em>stare decisis</em> operates.  Justice Scalia once confronted a similar argument for "disregard[ing] reliance interests" and showed how antithetical it was to rule-of-law principles.  <em>Quill Corp.</em> v. <em>North Dakota</em>, 504 U.&nbsp;S. 298, 320 (1992) (concurring opinion).  He noted first what we always tell lower courts: "If a precedent of this Court has direct application in a case, yet appears to rest on reasons rejected in some other line of decisions, [they] should follow the case which directly controls, leaving to this Court the prerogative of overruling its own decisions."  <em>Id., </em>at 321 (quoting <em>Rodriguez de Quijas</em> v. <em>Shearson/</em> <em>American Express, Inc.</em>, 490 U.&nbsp;S. 477, 484 (1989);  some alterations omitted).  That instruction, Justice Scalia explained, was "incompatible" with an expectation that "private parties anticipate our overrulings."  406 U.&nbsp;S., at 320.  He concluded: "[R]eliance upon a square, unabandoned holding of the Supreme Court is <em>always </em>justifiable reliance."  <em>Ibid.  Abood</em>'s holding was square.  It was unabandoned before today.  It was, in other words, the law--however much some were working overtime to make it not.  Parties, both unions and governments, were thus justified in relying on it.  And they did rely, to an extent rare among our decisions.  To dismiss the overthrowing of their settled expectations as entailing no more than some "adjustments" and "unpleasant transition costs," <em>ante,</em> at 47, is to trivialize <em>stare decisis.</em></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">IV</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;There is no sugarcoating today's opinion.  The majority overthrows a decision entrenched in this Nation's law--and in its economic life--for over 40 years.  As a result, it prevents the American people, acting through their state and local officials, from making important choices about workplace governance.  And it does so by weaponizing the First Amendment, in a way that unleashes judges, now and in the future, to intervene in economic and regulatory policy.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Departures from <em>stare decisis</em> are supposed to be "exceptional action[s]" demanding "special justification," <em>Rumsey</em>, 467 U.&nbsp;S., at 212--but the majority offers nothing like that here.  In contrast to the vigor of its attack on <em>Abood</em>, the majority's discussion of <em>stare decisis</em> barely limps to the finish line.  And no wonder: The standard factors this Court considers when deciding to overrule a decision all cut one way.  <em>Abood</em>'s legal underpinnings have not eroded over time: <em>Abood </em>is now, as it was when issued, consistent with this Court's First Amendment law.  <em>Abood </em>provided a workable standard for courts to apply.  And <em>Abood </em>has generated enormous reliance interests.  The majority has overruled <em>Abood </em>for no exceptional or special reason, but because it never liked the decision.  It has overruled <em>Abood </em>because it wanted to.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Because, that is, it wanted to pick the winning side in what should be--and until now, has been--an energetic policy debate.  Some state and local governments (and the constituents they serve) think that stable unions promote healthy labor relations and thereby improve the provision of services to the public.  Other state and local governments (and their constituents) think, to the contrary, that strong unions impose excessive costs and impair those services.  Americans have debated the pros and cons for many decades--in large part, by deciding whether to use fair-share arrangements.  Yesterday, 22 States were on one side, 28 on the other (ignoring a couple of in-betweeners).  Today, that healthy--that democratic--debate ends.  The majority has adjudged who should prevail.  Indeed, the majority is bursting with pride over what it has accomplished: Now those 22 States, it crows, "can follow the model of the federal government and 28 other States."  <em>Ante,</em> at 47, n. 27.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;And maybe most alarming, the majority has chosen the winners by turning the First Amendment into a sword, and using it against workaday economic and regulatory policy.  Today is not the first time the Court has wielded the First Amendment in such an aggressive way.  See, <em>e.g., National Institute of Family and Life Advocates </em>v. <em>Becerra</em>, <em>ante,</em> p. ___ (invalidating a law requiring medical and counseling facilities to provide relevant information to users); <em>Sorrell </em>v. <em>IMS Health Inc., </em>564 U.&nbsp;S. 552 (2011) (striking down a law that restricted pharmacies from selling various data).<em>  </em>And it threatens not to be the last.  Speech is everywhere--a part of every human activity (employment, health care, securities trading, you name it).  For that reason, almost all economic and regulatory policy affects or touches speech.  So the majority's road runs long.  And at every stop are black-robed rulers overriding citizens' choices.  The First Amendment was meant for better things.  It was meant not to undermine but to protect democratic governance--including over the role of public-sector unions.</p>
<p></p><hr>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The collective-action problem cited by the dissent, <em>post</em>, at 6, is not specific to the agency-fee context.  And contrary to the dissent's suggestion, it is often not practical for an entity that lobbies or advocates on behalf of the members of a group to tailor its message so that only its members benefit from its efforts.  Consider how effective it would be for a group that advocates on behalf of, say, seniors, to argue that a new measure should apply only to its dues-paying members.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In order to obtain that status, a union must petition to be recognized and campaign to win majority approval.  Ill. Comp. Stat., ch. 5, §315/9(a) (2016); see, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>County of Du Page</em> v. <em>Illinois Labor Relations Bd.</em>, 231 Ill. 2d 593, 597-600, 900 N.&nbsp;E.&nbsp;2d 1095, 1098-1099 (2008).  And unions eagerly seek this support.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, Brief for Employees of the State of Minnesota Court System as <em>Amici Curiae </em>9-17.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;There is precedent for such arrangements.  Some States have laws providing that, if an employee with a religious objection to paying an agency fee "requests the [union] to use the grievance procedure or arbitration procedure on the employee's behalf, the [union] is authorized to charge the employee for the reasonable cost of using such procedure."  <em>E.g.</em>,<em> </em>Cal. Govt. Code Ann. §3546.3 (West 2010); cf. Ill. Comp. Stat., ch. 5, §315/6(g) (2016).  This more tailored alternative, if applied to other objectors, would prevent free ridership while imposing a lesser burden on First Amendment rights.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Indeed, under common law, "collective bargaining was unlawful," <em>Teamsters</em> v. <em>Terry</em>, 494 U.&nbsp;S. 558, 565-566 (1990) (plurality opinion); see N. Citrine, Trade Union Law 4-7, 9-10 (2d ed. 1960); Notes, Legality of Trade Unions at Common Law, 25 Harv. L. Rev. 465, 466 (1912), and into the 20th century, every individual employee had the "liberty of contract" to "sell his labor upon such terms as he deem[ed] proper," <em>Adair</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 208 U.&nbsp;S. 161, 174-175 (1908); see R. Morris, Government and Labor in Early America 208, 529 (1946).  So even the concept of a private third-party entity with the power to bind employees on the terms of their employment likely would have been foreign to the Founders.  We note this only to show the problems inherent in the Union respondent's argument; we are not in any way questioning the foundations of modern labor law.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNopinion1.8" href="#FRopinion1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.</em>, Ellsworth, The Landholder, VII (1787), in Essays on the Constitution of the United States 167-171 (P. Ford ed. 1892); Webster, On Test Laws, Oaths of Allegiance and Abjuration, and Partial Exclusions from Office, in A Collection of Essays and Fugitiv[e] Writings 151-153 (1790).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNopinion1.9" href="#FRopinion1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Justice Powell's separate opinion did invoke <em>Pickering</em> in a relevant sense, but he did so only to acknowledge the State's relatively greater interest in regulating speech when it acts as employer than when it acts as sovereign.  <em>Abood</em> v. <em>Detroit Bd. of Ed.</em>, 431 U.&nbsp;S. 209, 259 (1977) (concurring in judgment).  In the very next sentence, he explained that "even in public employment, a significant impairment of First Amendment rights must survive exacting scrutiny."  <em>Ibid.</em> (internal quotation marks omitted).  That is the test we apply today.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNopinion1.10" href="#FRopinion1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Brief for State of Michigan et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae </em>9-24.  Nationwide, the cost of state and local employees' wages and benefits, for example, is nearly $1.5 trillion--more than half of those jurisdictions' total expenditures.  See Dept. of Commerce, Bureau of Economic Analysis, National Data, GDP &amp; Personal Income, Table 6.2D, line 92 (Aug. 3, 2017), and Table 3.3, line 37 (May 30, 2018), https://www.bea. gov/iTable/iTable.cfm?reqid=19&amp;step=2#reqid=19&amp;step=2&amp;isuri=1&amp;1921=survey.  And many States and cities struggle with unfunded pension and retiree healthcare liabilities and other budget issues.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNopinion1.11" href="#FRopinion1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;PEW Charitable Trusts, Fiscal 50: State Trends and Analysis (updated May 17, 2016), http://www.pewtrusts.org/en/research-and-analysis/data-visualizations/2014/fiscal-50#ind4.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNopinion1.12" href="#FRopinion1.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Brief for Jason R. Barclay et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 9; M. Egan, How Illinois Became America's Most Messed-Up State, CNN Money (July 1, 2017), https://cnnmon.ie/2tp9NX5.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 13" name="FNopinion1.13" href="#FRopinion1.13">13</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Brief for Jason R. Barclay et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 9.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 14" name="FNopinion1.14" href="#FRopinion1.14">14</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;E. Campbell, S&amp;P, Moody's Downgrade Illinois to Near Junk, Lowest Ever for a U.&nbsp;S. State, Bloomberg (June 1, 2017), https:// bloom.bg/2roEJUc.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 15" name="FNopinion1.15" href="#FRopinion1.15">15</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See National Association of State Budget Officers, Summary: Spring 2018 Fiscal Survey of States 2 (June 14, 2018), http://www.nasbo.org; ProQuest Statistical Abstract of the United States: 2018, pp.&nbsp;306, Table&nbsp;476, 321, Table&nbsp;489.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 16" name="FNopinion1.16" href="#FRopinion1.16">16</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Rogers, School Districts 'Race to the Top' Despite Teacher Dispute, Marin Independent J., June 19, 2010.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 17" name="FNopinion1.17" href="#FRopinion1.17">17</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Sawchuk, Transferring Top Teachers Has Benefits: Study Probes Moving Talent to Low-Performing Schools, Education Week, Nov. 13, 2013, pp.&nbsp;1, 13.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 18" name="FNopinion1.18" href="#FRopinion1.18">18</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Tucker, Textbooks Equivocate on Global Warming: Stanford Study Finds Portrayal 'Dishonest,' San Francisco Chronicle, Nov. 24, 2015, p.&nbsp;C1.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 19" name="FNopinion1.19" href="#FRopinion1.19">19</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Reagan, Anti-Confederacy Movement Rekindles Texas Textbook Controversy, San Antonio Current, Aug. 4, 2015.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 20" name="FNopinion1.20" href="#FRopinion1.20">20</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Watanabe, How To Teach Gay Issues in 1st Grade? A New Law Requiring California Schools To Have Lessons About LGBT Americans Raises Tough Questions, L.&nbsp;A. Times, Oct. 16, 2011, p.&nbsp;A1.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 21" name="FNopinion1.21" href="#FRopinion1.21">21</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Goodstein, A Web of Faith, Law and Science in Evolution Suit, N.&nbsp;Y. Times, Sept. 26, 2005, p.&nbsp;A1.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 22" name="FNopinion1.22" href="#FRopinion1.22">22</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Golden, Defending the Faith: New Battleground in Textbook Wars: Religion in History, Wall St. J., Jan. 25, 2006, p.&nbsp;A1.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 23" name="FNopinion1.23" href="#FRopinion1.23">23</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Claiming that our decision will hobble government operations, the dissent asserts that it would prevent a government employer from taking action against disruptive non-unionized employees in two carefully constructed hypothetical situations.  See <em>post</em>, at 17-18.  Both hypotheticals are short on potentially important details, but in any event, neither would be affected by our decision in this case.  Rather, both would simply call for the application of the standard <em>Pickering</em> test.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In one of the hypotheticals, teachers "protest merit pay in the school cafeteria."  <em>Post</em>, at 17.  If such a case actually arose, it would be important to know, among other things, whether the teachers involved were supposed to be teaching in their classrooms at the time in question and whether the protest occurred in the presence of students during the student lunch period.  If both those conditions were met, the teachers would presumably be violating content-neutral rules regarding their duty to teach at specified times and places, and their conduct might well have a disruptive effect on the educational process.  Thus, in the dissent's hypothetical, the school's interests might well outweigh those of the teachers, but in this hypothetical case, as in all <em>Pickering</em> cases, the particular facts would be very important.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In the other hypothetical, employees agitate for a better health plan "at various inopportune times and places."  <em>Post</em>, at 17.  Here, the lack of factual detail makes it impossible to evaluate how the <em>Pickering</em> balance would come out.  The term "agitat[ion]" can encompass a wide range of conduct, as well as speech.  <em>Post</em>, at 17.  And the time and place of the agitation would also be important.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 24" name="FNopinion1.24" href="#FRopinion1.24">24</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;No First Amendment issue could have properly arisen in those cases unless Congress's enactment of a provision allowing, but not requiring, private parties to enter into union-shop arrangements was sufficient to establish governmental action.  That proposition was debatable when <em>Abood</em> was decided, and is even more questionable today.  See <em>American Mfrs. Mut. Ins. Co.</em> v. <em>Sullivan</em>, 526 U.&nbsp;S. 40, 53 (1999); <em>Jackson</em> v. <em>Metropolitan Edison Co.</em>, 419 U.&nbsp;S. 345, 357 (1974).  Compare, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>White</em> v. <em>Communications Workers of Am., AFL-CIO, Local 13000</em>, 370 F.&nbsp;3d 346, 350 (CA3 2004) (no state action), and <em>Kolinske</em> v. <em>Lubbers</em>, 712 F.&nbsp;2d 471, 477-478 (CADC 1983) (same), with <em>Beck</em> v. <em>Communications Workers of Am.</em>, 776 F.&nbsp;2d 1187, 1207 (CA4 1985) (state action), and <em>Linscott</em> v. <em>Millers Falls Co.</em>, 440 F.&nbsp;2d 14, 16, and n. 2 (CA1 1971) (same).  We reserved decision on this question in <em>Communications Workers</em> v. <em>Beck</em>, 487 U.&nbsp;S. 735, 761 (1988), and do not resolve it here.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 25" name="FNopinion1.25" href="#FRopinion1.25">25</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Contrary to the dissent's claim, see <em>post</em>, at 19, and n.&nbsp;4, the fact that "[t]he rationale of [<em>Abood</em>] does not withstand careful analysis" <em>is</em> a reason to overrule it, <em>e.g.</em>,<em> Lawrence</em> v. <em>Texas</em>, 539 U.&nbsp;S. 558, 577 (2003).  And that is even truer when, as here, the defenders of the precedent do not attempt to "defend [its actual] reasoning."  <em>Citizens United</em> v. <em>Federal Election Comm'n</em>, 558 U.&nbsp;S. 310, 363 (2010); <em>id.</em>, at 382-385 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Roberts, C.&nbsp;J.</small></span>, concurring).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 26" name="FNopinion1.26" href="#FRopinion1.26">26</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;For this reason, it is hardly surprising that chargeability issues have not arisen in many Court of Appeals cases.  See <em>post</em>, at 22 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kagan, J.,</small></span> dissenting).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 27" name="FNopinion1.27" href="#FRopinion1.27">27</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The dissent emphasizes another type of reliance, namely, that "[o]ver 20 States have by now enacted statutes authorizing [agency-fee] provisions."  <em>Post</em>, at 23.  But as we explained in <em>Citizens United</em>,<em> </em>"[t]his is not a compelling interest for <em>stare decisis</em>.  If it were, legislative acts could prevent us from overruling our own precedents, thereby interfering with our duty 'to say what the law is.'&nbsp;"  558 U.&nbsp;S., at 365 (quoting <em>Marbury</em> v. <em>Madison</em>, 1 Cranch 137, 177 (1803)).  Nor does our decision "&nbsp;'require an extensive legislative response.'&nbsp;"  <em>Post</em>, at 23.  States can keep their labor-relations systems exactly as they are--only they cannot force nonmembers to subsidize public-sector unions.  In this way, these States can follow the model of the federal government and 28 other States.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 28" name="FNopinion1.28" href="#FRopinion1.28">28</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Unfortunately, the dissent sees the need to resort to accusations that we are acting like "black-robed rulers" who have shut down an "energetic policy debate."  <em>Post</em>, at 27-28.  We certainly agree that judges should not "overrid[e] citizens' choices" or "pick the winning side," <em>ibid.</em>--unless the Constitution commands that they do so.  But when a federal or state law violates the Constitution, the American doctrine of judicial review requires us to enforce the Constitution.  Here, States with agency-fee laws have abridged fundamental free speech rights.  In holding that these laws violate the Constitution, we are simply enforcing the First Amendment as properly understood, "[t]he very purpose of [which] was to withdraw certain subjects from the vicissitudes of political controversy, to place them beyond the reach of majorities and officials and to establish them as legal principles to be applied by the courts."  <em>West Virginia Bd. of Ed.</em> v. <em>Barnette</em>, 319 U.&nbsp;S. 624, 638 (1943).</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority relies on statistics from the federal workforce (where agency fees are unlawful) to suggest that public employees do not act in accord with economic logic.  See <em>ante,</em> at 12.  But first, many fewer federal employees pay dues than have voted for a union to represent them, indicating that free-riding in fact pervades the federal sector.  See, <em>e.g., </em>R. Kearney &amp; P. Mareschal, Labor Relations in the Public Sector 26 (5th ed. 2014).  And second, that sector is not typical of other public workforces.  Bargaining in the federal sphere is limited; most notably, it does not extend to wages and benefits.  See <em>Fort Stewart Schools</em> v. <em>FLRA</em>, 495 U.&nbsp;S. 641, 649 (1990).  That means union operating expenses are lower than they are elsewhere.  And the gap further widens because the federal sector uses large, often national, bargaining units that provide unions with economies of scale.  See Brief for International Brotherhood of Teamsters as <em>Amicus Curiae</em> 7.  For those reasons, the federal workforce is the wrong place to look for meaningful empirical evidence on the issues here.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;For those reasons, it is not surprising that the "categorization schemes" in <em>Abood </em>and <em>Pickering</em> are not precisely coterminous.  <em>Ante,</em> at 25.  The two cases<em> </em>are fraternal rather than identical twins--both standing for the proposition that the government receives great deference when it regulates speech as an employer rather than as a sovereign.  See <em>infra</em> this page and 12-13.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;That's why this Court has blessed the constitutionality of compelled speech subsidies in a variety of cases beyond <em>Abood</em>, involving a variety of contexts beyond labor relations.  The list includes mandatory fees imposed on state bar members (for professional expression); university students (for campus events); and fruit processors (for generic advertising).  See <em>Keller</em> v. <em>State Bar of Cal.</em>, 496 U.&nbsp;S. 1, 14 (1990); <em>Board of Regents of Univ. of Wis. System</em> v. <em>Southworth</em>, 529 U.&nbsp;S. 217, 233 (2000); <em>Glickman</em> v. <em>Wileman Brothers &amp; Elliott, Inc.</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S. 457, 474 (1997); see also <em>infra</em>, at 20.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;And then, after ostensibly turning to <em>stare decisis</em>, the majority spends another four pages insisting that <em>Abood </em>was "not well reasoned," which is just more of the same.  <em>Ante,</em> at 38; see <em>ante,</em> at 35-38.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Indeed, some agency-fee provisions, if canceled, could bring down entire contracts because they lack severability clauses.  See <em>ante,</em> at 46 (noting that unions could have negotiated for that result); Brief for Governor Tom Wolf et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 11.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In a single, cryptic sentence, the majority also claims that arguments about reliance "based on [<em>Abood</em>'s] clarity<em> </em>are misplaced" because <em>Abood </em>did not provide a "clear or easily applicable standard" to separate fees for collective bargaining from those for political activities.  <em>Ante,</em> at 45.  But to begin, the standard for separating those activities was clear and workable, as I have already shown.  See <em>supra,</em> at 21-22.  And in any event, the reliance <em>Abood </em>engendered was based not on the clarity of that line, but on the clarity of its holding that governments and unions could generally agree to fair-share arrangements.</p>
 
        <p></p>
        <p></p>

    <div class="case-information">
        <h2 class="case-information__title"> JANUS<em> v. AMERICAN FEDERATION OF STATE, COUNTY, AND MUNICIPAL EMPLOYEES,</em> COUNCIL 31, <span style="TEXT-TRANSFORM: UPPERCASE;"><small>ET AL.</small></span> (2018)</h2>
        
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Docket No:</b> No. 16-1466</p>
        
        
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Argued:</b> February 26, 2018</p>
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Decided:</b> June 27, 2018</p>
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Court:</b> United States Supreme Court</p>
    </div>

</main><script>_satellite["_runScript1"](function(event, target, Promise) {
_satellite.ads = {};

_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory = function(nameString, href) {
  // TODO remove this once the correct PA is being used for the lid
  if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') && _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa ) {
    nameString = nameString.replace(/pa:[0-9]+/, "pa:"+_satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa);  
  }
  _satellite.logger.debug('_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory nameString: '+nameString);

  // sample name: "&lid=clk:fs/prod:dts/cid:5088127/msa:259/pa:1313/sub:3107462/pos:1"
  var s = nameString;
  s = s.substr(5);
  var lid = s;
  s = s.replaceAll(':','":"');
  s = s.replaceAll('/','","');
  s = '{"'+s+'"}'
  var adInfo = JSON.parse(s);
  adInfo.lid = lid;
  adInfo.paid = (typeof adInfo.sub != 'undefined');
  adInfo.adId = (adInfo.paid ? adInfo.sub : adInfo.cid);
  var map = {
    'ph':'phone',
    'fs':'website',
    'pv':'profile',
    'cu':'contact',
    'vr':'review-read',
    'wr':'review-write',
    'sl':'super-lawyers'
  };  
  adInfo.type = map[adInfo.clk];   
  adInfo.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id')+'-'+adInfo.adId+'-'+(new Date().getTime());
  
  if( href && adInfo.type == 'phone' ) {
    adInfo.detail = href.replace(/[^\d]/g,'');
    if( adInfo.detail.length > 10 ) {
      adInfo.detail = adInfo.detail.substr(adInfo.detail.length-10);
    }
  }  
  
  return adInfo;
}

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];
  }
  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

_satellite.ads.sendConversionFindLaw = function() {
  var subId = _satellite.getVar('current_ad_eng').sub;
  var cookieName = 'fl_ad_'+subId;
  var cookieValue = 'true';
  var c = _satellite.cookie.get(cookieName);
  // we use a cookie to ensure multiple beacons are not sent for the same ad
  if( typeof c == 'undefined' ) {
    _satellite.setFlCookie(cookieName, cookieValue, 1);

    var data = {};
    var clickUrlElement = document.getElementById(_satellite.getVar('ad_eng_sub'));
    if (clickUrlElement !== null) {
      data.clickUrl = clickUrlElement.innerText;
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') ) {
      data.adDecisionContext = _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_transaction_id') ) {
      data.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_transaction_id');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_sub') ) {
      data.subId = _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_sub');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id') ) {
      data.flUserId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('fl_tag_track') == 'false' ) {
      data.internalFlag = 'true'; // mimick as an internal request
    }

    let url = new URL(window.location.href);
    let domain = 'attorneys.findlaw'+(_satellite.getVar('cms_env') == 'prod' ? '' : '-stage')+'.com';
    let attorneysUrl = new URL('https://'+domain+'/flstats/v1/stats');
    let searchParams = new URLSearchParams(window.location.search);

    if (searchParams.has('ads-server-env')) {
      attorneysUrl.searchParams.set('ads-server-env', url.searchParams.get('ads-server-env'));
    }
    navigator.sendBeacon(attorneysUrl.href, JSON.stringify(data));
  }
}

    expireDays = dt.toGMTString();
  }
  document.cookie = cookieName+"="+cookieValue+"; expires="+expireDays+'; path='+path+'; domain='+_satellite.getVar('host_root');
};

        var pageType = _satellite.getVar("page_page_type");
        if(pageType == "article") toStore += " Article";

      _satellite.writeCrossSubdomainCookie("s_dsource", toStore);

    _satellite.slowDown = function(e, goTo){
      e.preventDefault(); // prevent default anchor behavior
      setTimeout(function() {
        window.location = goTo;
      }, 300);
    }
    
    //CTA Search - enter & click
    var nextStepsForm = document.querySelector('.nextsteps form');
    nextStepsForm.addEventListener('submit', function(ev) {
      _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Next Steps");

        if (e.key === 'Enter') {
          if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/")
            _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
          else
            _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
        }
      });

      submitButton,

      searchSecondaryInput
    ];
    for (var i = 0; i < submitButtons.length; i++) {
      var button = submitButtons[i];
      button.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        if (location.host + location.pathname === "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
        } else {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
        }
      });
    }

    //Homepage search
    if (location.host + location.pathname === "www.findlaw.com/") {
      var forms = document.querySelectorAll("form[action*='stdSearch_process.jsp']");
      for (var i = 0; i < forms.length; i++) {
        var form = forms[i];
        form.addEventListener('submit', function() {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Home");
        });
      }
    }

    //CTA
    var links = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="fli="], .articleHeading p a[href*="lawyers.findlaw.com"]');

        var source = link.dataset.searchLocation || '';

          if (link.closest('.cta-public').length > 0) {
            source = 'CTA: Banner';
          } else {
            source = 'CTA: Inline Link';
          }
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        }
      });

        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);

            source = "Browse: State";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statecounty/") > -1) {

        var goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
        var source = $(this).data("search-location") || $(this).parents("[data-search-location]").data("search-location");
        _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
      });
    }

  } catch (exp) {
    _satellite.logger.log("Issue with 'Search Source' Rule");
  }
  
});
});</script><script>
function setTrackerCookie(name, value)
{
  var hostParts = location.host.split('.').reverse();
  var cookieDomain = '.' + hostParts[1] + '.' + hostParts[0];
  document.cookie = name + '=' + value + ';domain=' + cookieDomain + ';path=/';
}
